With Summit Films taking a page from Warner Bros. treatment of the final Harry Potter movie, this film represents 50% of the last hurrah of the Twilight Saga and it enters territory previously unexplored in the past three films. In a typical review I would warn you that spoilers may follow, but any information I'm about to divulge about the plot seems to be common knowledge. I have never read the books or actively tried to have the story spoiled for me, but I also don't live under a rock and knew many plot details beforehand. Breaking Dawn opens with characters receiving invitations to the wedding of Bella Swan (Kristen Stewart) and Edward Cullen (Robert Pattinson). The first person we see receiving an invitation is Jacob (Taylor Lautner), who storms out of his house upon hearing the news and before you know it...There goes the shirt. Yes, the long awaited human/vampire union has arrived and goes off mostly without a hitch. Minutes later, Edward and Bella have arrived on a private island in Brazil for their honeymoon. Here, the abstinence metaphor of the story is disregarded in favor of providing audiences with the most long-awaited onscreen deflowering in years. I warn you not to be too intrigued by this prospect as the \\"sex scene\\" is a total cop out. When the core audience that has made millionaires of many studio executives is under the age of seventeen, what can be shown is significantly restricted. In Breaking Dawn, the moment of truth is a swift cut to the next day where the room is in shambles and Bella is covered in bruises. I almost felt bad for the Twilight fans. They waited so long for...this?  Of course, the plot really begins when Bella discovers that she's pregnant with Edward's vampiric spawn and the fetus is growing at an accelerated rate that prompts them to rush back to Forks, Washington to be watched over by the Cullen Clan. Turns out that no one was aware a human could be impregnated by a vampire and that Bella's pregnancy will likely result in her death. Not satisfied with one dramatic conflict, Bella's pregnancy ends the peace treaty between vampires and werewolves making it Jacob's responsibility to dissent from his pack and help protect Bella.   After three years and three films of heavy petting, you get marriage, sex, and pregnancy all within an hour. Having previously been an allegory for abstinence, Twilight now succeeds at being an allegory for how quickly a young girl can ruin her life. The bizarre plot of Breaking Dawn works in its favor, making it more mature and compelling than its predecessors although its flaws simply cannot be overlooked. While the acting has improved exponentially, the dialogue has not and the visual effects are still piss poor. After three films, Pattinson has grown as an actor and no longer looking nauseated in every scene, actually manages to give Edward some depth of character. Lautner's boyishness hinders his performance in that it makes it hard to accept him as a masculine romantic lead. His determination and obsession with Bella make you want to reassure him \\"everything's going to be OK, scamp\\" rather than scream \\"yeah, bro!\\" Stewart, a usually capable actress in non-Twilight material, makes Bella more tolerable than ever but fails to make some of the film's most important scenes convincing. During the wedding scene, Bella doesn't look excited or nervous or jittery. She looks sick with a chronic stomachache. Meanwhile, Bella's pregnant look seems to be modeled after the people on \\"Meth Project\\" billboards. I don't need Twilight fans to explain the logistics behind her pregnancy; I'm aware that it's responsible for making her look that way. Pregnant belly aside, Bella seems to be in the throes of crystal meth addiction rather than suffering through a vampire pregnancy. The most embarrassing piece of acting comes from the pack of werewolves having a meeting in their werewolf form. The poorly-rendered CGI-wolves snarling at each other while the laughable voice performances sound lazily frustrated make this scene unintentionally humorous. Ironically, one of the most consistent aspects of all four films is Billy Burke as Charlie Swan. As in the previous entries, Burke is effortless and charming, continuing his trek through these movies unscathed. He is so natural and likable amidst the chaos that it makes his performance seem Oscar-worthy. His speech at the wedding actually made me smile; one of the few times I've ever smiled during these films for the right reasons.  Despite reportedly being a real-life couple, there remains a noticeable lack of chemistry between Stewart and Pattinson. Since this is where Bella officially, with no regrets, chooses Edward over Jacob I wonder why Stewart and Lautner have more chemistry and emotional connection. One of the most poignant moments of the whole saga occurs in a scene between Bella and Jacob after the wedding. Is this a reflection of the acting or is this how the story was written? The latter would make sense. I've given up trying to criticize the particulars of the story. I have many questions, beginning with why the Cullen Clan puts up with the immature girl with no personality, but my biggest question is about the logistics behind the sparkling. I don't know it works, but I know there are several scenes depicting Edward in broad daylight...And he's not sparkling. Did the filmmakers forget? Or am I just ignorant to the ways of the sparkle?  I have not read the books, but I now feel comfortable placing any blame on my qualms with the storytelling and/or dialogue on Meyer rather than screenwriter Melissa Rosenberg. I think Rosenberg does the best she can with the little freedom she has to tamper with the original text. I put bias aside watching these films, but I'll admit that I have no faith in the quality or intelligence of the writing. There's a moment of Edward and Bella playing chess, Bella making her game-ending move and exclaiming \\"I win!\\" The possibility that this wasn't the line she was passively given and that the writer had no idea what \\"checkmate\\" was did occur to me.  It's common knowledge that Meyer is Mormon, but nothing has made that more apparent than Breaking Dawn. When Bella is pregnant, the story becomes, with no subtlety, an allegory for the evils of abortion. The most uninformed, absent-minded viewer would be able to pick up on this. Edward alludes to abortion by saying they need to \\"get that thing out\\" and a character later scolds another for saying `fetus' with \\"Say it! It's a baby.\\" It's hard to ignore the anti-choice stance made when Bella, knowing her pregnancy will likely kill her, decides to go through with it. I don't believe the quality of a film is affected by it taking a stance on a controversial issue, whether that stance is parallel to my own or not, I just wish they could have been a little more subtle with their handling of this issue. I'm aware the Twilight Saga has never been much for subtlety, you've heard the dialogue, but the message is a little too heavy-handed and draws more attention toitself than necessary.  Breaking Dawn is very well shot, particularly in regards to the locale of the honeymoon, and it boasts a professional feel that the series lacked previously. Condon has not changed water into wine but his proficiency makes the long-awaited birth scene into a surprisingly harrowing moment, even without the restrictions considered. It's a well-edited and assembled scene that is certainly one of the most impressive in the movie. The execution of the final scene is actually effective too, an achievement I fully attribute to Condon as it's not what's being shown but how it's being shown that ultimately matters. Even with these moments of success, his handling of the imprinting scene is a low point. I don't know who could have handled this in a convincingly serious manner, but it plays like a bad joke and could possibly be the most embarrassing moment of Condon's career, as well as Lautner's.  Each Twilight film has been progressively better than the previous entry, or perhaps less terrible. Breaking Dawn skates close to the edge of being an acceptable movie, with the writing being what ultimately holds it back. Even with a knowledgeable director, compelling scenes, and actors who finally seem to care, it just can't rise above the foundation of Meyer's putrid writing. I admit that, despite how many times I rolled my eyes, I almost liked Breaking Dawn. For the first time with this series, I was entertained enough to say \\"hey, that was acceptable for the emotionally stagnant melodrama it is.\\" I did not think it was almost a good film, I did not almost understand its cultural impact, nor did I almost embrace the characters or their plight. With Eclipse, the Twilight Saga became more tolerable by morphing into an action film. Breaking Dawn becomes more tolerable by (almost) morphing into a horror film. I stand by my assertion that no one years from now will look back on these films fondly and I believe that the popularity of this series will completelydie out with the release of the final movie next year. I found Breaking Dawn compelling enough at times and it entertained me, but it's still a mediocre and poorly written mess. Eclipse was better than its predecessors in the sense that HIV is better than AIDS. With Breaking Dawn, it's better than its predecessors in the sense that it's like herpes...At least it's not HIV.  GRADE: C

0393064476|5.0|Readers may note that my review of WILL IN THE WORLD shows I am a fan of this author, but still, I didn't think he would manage to write another book that delighted, moved, and stimulated me to that extent.  With THE SWERVE (note: weird title, even though he explains it), he does.  When I was young and foolish, I adored Hermann Hesse's THE GLASS BEAD GAME for its concept of a world where science and art, philosophy and physics, religious impulse and joie de vivre, all somehow mixed together and became a dependable course of learning.  I am one of those who believe that when we stop learning, we die, as well as a closet Classicist and a lover of the very concept of Western renaissance.  So how could I not adore this book, which reminds me of the Bead Game in that every page brings together several things I hadn't thought of and makes sense of them?  We start with one of a handful of surviving copies from the shattered Classical world of Lucretius' \\"On the Nature of Things,\\" almost the only surviving display of Epicurian thought - and if you think \\"eat, drink and be merry\\" sums that philosophy up, you are required to read this book.  Again and again Greenblatt, in the midst of a fun almost-mystery-thriller story of rediscovering the lost masterpiece of Lucretius, mixes it all up and throws down the dice and - voila! - I've learned something new, see something different, find a pattern I had not expected.  The joyous, non-Christian, atomic, poetic, profoundly curious notions of Epicurus slowly filtered into a fundamentalist world of Christian thought and, like a pebble in the lake, began to change everything.  Yes, it is a strain to say Lucretius' and Epicurus' atoms led to the Renaissance, but the argument is made with deft and passionate scholarship.  So maybe . . .?  This is, to me, the best kind of intellectual exhilaration and it's very rarely found.  It doesn't matter if you don't know a thing about either Lucretius or dear Poggio or Botticcelli, if you understand the rise of science, the fall of fundamentalism, the moves of nations.  If you believe that the concept of individual human achievement and the life of the mind is important, READ THIS BOOK and enjoy his dancing prose.  You'll be thinking about it long after the book is done.

B000059MEK|5.0|Known for their French-styled House music as well as Dance/Electronica music with unique, live performances that include various visual effects,  are an electronic music duo formed in 1993 that includes French musicians Guy-Manuel de Homem-Christo and Thomas Bangalter. The French duo started getting attention during House movement in late 1990s with their innovative style and became popular worldwide with their debut album Homework, which was released in 1997. Daft Punk's second studio album released in March 2001, Discovery turned out to be a bigger hit and has major hit tracks such as \\"Digital Love\\", \\"One More Time\\", and \\"Harder, Better, Faster, Stronger\\". In the album, Daft Punk effectively incorporates a wide range of styles and samples, while creating a pop-culture mix of funky music.          The duo Daft Punk was largely influenced from musical works by the The Rolling Stones, The Beach Boys, and Elton John. In their works, Daft Punk often use disco techniques from various musicians such as electronic music giant , and have created one of the most unique house beats in modern pop-culture history.  According to the artist himself, de Homem-Christo usually plays the guitar while Bangalter performs on keyboards or bass guitar. The duo's ability to play multiple instruments adds dynamic element to the 7-time Grammy award nominees and 2-time Grammy award winning group.         Discovery is distinctively different from the previous album. Homework is often described as a \\"party\\" album that has fun and energetic songs. Even though the album does not have heavy-disco element to it, it certainly elevates listeners' energy with its combination of dance beats with electronic and funky music. Discovery, on the other hand, accounts for unique synthpop-oriented style and focuses on reconnecting with lively childhood memories as it features many samples from the late 1970's and 80's music. At its peak, the album gained popularity as it took the number two spot in Britishmainstream music.        The first single of the album, \\"One More Time\\", was released on November 13th, 2000. The song highly features a heavy auto-tuned and compressed vocal performance sung by an American producer . With frequent alterations using Auto-Tune, Romanthony heightens the electronic element that is blended with the funkiness of the music. The song was a huge success by the French Duo. In addition to accomplishment in the United Kingdom in 2000, \\"One More Time\\" was one of Daft Punk's few charting songs in the United States, reaching number 61 on the Billboard Hot 100. Recently, the song was featured in the Wii video game Boogie and PlayStation 3's SingStar.    The third single from the album, \\"Digital Love\\", was another homerun by the French duo. It was so popular that the song was featured in the film Interstella 5555: The 5tory of the 5ecret 5tar 5ystem, a Japanese animated musical film released in 2003. During production, a Puerto Rican musician DJ Sneak composed the lyrics that were performed by Daft Punk. \\"Digital Love\\" is known for the guitar solo performed by Bangalter, who admitted using music sequencers in the making. The song gained popularity worldwide, as it charted 14th place on the UK Singles Chart and made number nine on the Hot Dance Music/Club Play chart in the United States.      Another hit in the album, \\"Harder, Better, Faster, Stronger\\" also features heavy, robotic, auto-tuned vocals, and it contains a sample of \\"Cola Bottle Baby\\" by Edwin Birdsong. The song was remixed by Kanye West, who used a vocal sample from the original and named his new track \\"Stronger.\\" The remixed version also received high praise as it won a Grammy Award at the 50th Grammy Awards, where Daft Punk made a surprise appearance to congratulate West.    In addition to \\"Harder, Better, Faster, Stronger,\\" a couple of other tracks from the album were sampled by other musicians. Wiley's song \\"Summertime\\" from the album See Clear Now includes a sample of \\"Aerodynamic.\\" Also, Jazmine Sullivan's song \\"Dream Big\\" from the album Fearless features a part of \\"Veridis Quo.\\"         Throughout the album, there are many samples from the early works by other musicians. Instead of making new music using those samples, Daft Punk simply edited them by adding new instrumental performance. In their second studio album, the French duo did what they do best by creating French disco house music that captured millions of people's attention over the world. Overall, the album has gained global recognition as manifested in sales and charts. In 2005, it was estimated that Discovery has sold at least 2.6 million copies as of 2005. Furthermore, Rhapsody ranked the album at number 12 on its \\"100 Best Albums of the Decade\\" list in 2009.

B001OB7F98|5.0|Bjork's Post is an album that mixes various musical styles but is largely built on the electronic-pop of the first album. Probably the most defining aspect of this album is it's use of technology in producing the sounds and textures prevalent throughout. This is done in various ways on different tracks and can be seen especially in the songs \\"Army of Me\\" and \\"Isobel.\\"  \\"Army of Me\\" is the first song on the album and immediately propels the listener into Bjork's musical world. The song is loud and powerful, making it a jarring introduction to the album. Exceptional use of technology helps it to achieve this sound. From the first note the song makes use of sound effects and electronic instruments. The song lyrics are meant to be arousing and provocative, almost sounding angry. The music fits this mood very well. The consistent low drone of the base line in combination with the decrescendos of electronic sound serve this purpose well. It is a very good song that introduces the type of instrumentation that will be used throughout the album but is still very unique in it's composition.  \\"Isobel\\" is a song further along in the album that expertly employs the use of technology in a very different way than in \\"Army of Me.\\" \\"Isobel\\" can best be described as more low-key and disquieting than other songs on the album. At the start, it sounds as if it is going to be a more classically based song but then shifts with a repetitive drum beat and various electronic instruments, including some sort of synthesizer and keyboard. These sounds add a beautiful sound that is somewhat disquieting, dark feel. This goes well with the lyrics involving nature and a woman isolated from the world. This song does well to complement the album, doing well to mix modern technological sounds with an outdoorsy drum beat and more classical strings.  Bjork is very much intent on the use of technology to achieve a very appealing cacophony of sound that fits well with her lyrics and other instruments. These sounds create a veritable world of intense sound that is meant to allow the listener to appreciate the environment. Often this environment is intended to help musically express a natural surrounding. In a quote on her website, Bjork states:   Just as we use music to express parts of us that would otherwise be hidden, so to can we use technology to make visible much of nature's invisible world.... You will see how the three come together: nature, music, and technology. (Bjork.com)  Bjork's emphasis on the use of technology for expression within a musical piece is important to her and is made evident by her use of it in her second album Post.

Being one of the mainstream electronic music artists, Justice may not be considered as much experimenting with computer music as scholarly electronic musicians because they are probably more interested in making accessible and club friendly songs, which people can enjoy and have fun with them. However, what Justice can do with music and their great songs definitely have something to contribute to the development of computer music.

B002I0J5FG|2.0|I played both of these games back when they first came out on the PS2 and remember kinda enjoying them. Given all the effulgent gushing and kiss-blowing from so many other reviewers on various websites, I felt like I probably enjoyed both games more than I had remembered, and ended up buying this \\"remastered\\" collection. Turns out, I guess I had it backward, as both ICO and SOC ended up being very frustrating game experiences for me, each in their own ways.   ICO   There exists a small group of dedicated and vocal gamers who seem to really like the concept of videogames as art. While I don't have a problem with the fundamental idea (I loved Okami), I do have a major problem with development studios who hide poor game and programming design behind artsy camera angles and atmosphere, and try to pass it off as a videogame masterpiece. After having played through ICO (and also SOC) my opinion of Team ICO is that they are obviously very interested in creating art, but lack the technical knowlege required to craft a playable video game around it, and ultimately would be better off shooting an independent film or painting a series of pictures and then staring at them pensively.    Did I like anything about ICO? Yeah, I like the environmental puzzles, where the goal is always to figure out how to open a door or reach a certain ledge, and most of the solutions are logical (as environmental puzzles should be). That's about it though, as just about everything else in the game frustrated me almost to the point of quitting early (I didn't though, as I wanted to unlock all the trophies).   The things that crippled the game experience for me were the controls, the camera, the friendly AI, and the horribly boring and repetitive fights. The controls lack responsiveness and feel sluggish. Grabbing a hold of ladders or dangling chains is often far more difficult than it needs to be, and if a torch and switch are next to each other, it's not unusual to see your character grab the one you don't intend for him to grab. Repeatedly. The controls are also constantly locked in mortal combat with the semi-fixed camera angles. What this means is since your character moves in direct relation to the direction you're pushing, if you're holding right and running down the hallway and the camera decides to swing more to the right to face you, you'll end up running into the wall since the wall is now directly to your right and that's still the direction you're pushing. It's lazy programming, and most other third person game developers have figured out how to deal with this irritation. The camera also has the annoying habit of swivelling around to face your character as he's moving, so that you can't see what's coming up without using the right analog stick to move the entire camera frame to the right (the only real camera control the game allows the player), leaving your character out of the picture, and providing a terribly disorienting angle on the gameplay. It's just bad design, and no other game that I can think of has this set-up.   The friendly AI is an abject failure of videogame programming. What more can be said? Yorda has serious issues with following commands, and it's all too common to see her struggle with climbing onto ladders or even continuing to climb up ladders without inexplicably reversing tack and climbing back down. She can't drop down a 4 foot ledge in one room, but she can drop down a different 4 food ledge in another room. And God forbid you get in a hurry, as Yorda has the nasty habit of strolling along when you need her to run quickly (like in battles). The whole thing is incredibly irritating and makes it feel like your main goal of the entire game is to babysit a slow child... which I guess it pretty much is.   Battles are boring and one dimensional and involve wailing on wave after wave of shadow creature with your trusty 2x4 or sword. Some battles can be avoided, fortunately, but avoidance frequently leads you into an even greaterbattle as the controls and camera once again tagteam your escape efforts. Honestly, the battles should've just been removed. They add nothing to the game and only detract from it.   Can't comment on the music since there really isn't any. If you get a hard-on listening to endless feet clacking on stone, or wind rustling, or the odd phrase \\"bon swah\\" repeated over and over and OVER AGAIN, then you're in luck.   I can't help but feel like this game would be better served as a downloadable title for like $10 max.  SHADOW OF THE COLOSSUS   I spent more text than I thought I would describing ICO, so I'll keep the SOC part briefer. Overall, it's a better game than ICO, but it has its very own set of design flaws which seriously maimed a game experience that should otherwise be amazing. There is little doubt that Team ICO made this game, given their efforts with ICO.   You have full camera control this time around, but it's not what I thought it'd be. Most third person games provide the player with limited camera control in which the game keeps the camera behind your character and moves accordingly as you move your character, but still allows you to move the camera yourself if necessary. In SOC, there is no automatic camera control by the game. What this means is that if you're doing more than moving in a straight line, you're going to have to have one thumb on the right analog stick moving it AT ALL TIMES. Take a break for a second, and you'll find your character moving to the side of the screen or facing you, providing you with absolutely no warning of what's coming up. Pull out your bow while running toward the screen (toward you) and your character aims/shoots at you instead of the colossus in the background (which is indescribably backward compared to other games in which your character usually immediately faces the enemy). Controlling the horse while using the camera is also bizarrely overcomplicated and has you doing thumb aerobics switching back and forth betweenthe X button for \\"speed up horse\\" and the right thumb stick for camera control. The control/camera set-up is poorly thought out and should have been simplified.... especially for a game that prides itself on its relaxed atmosphere.    The controls (like ICO) are sluggish and don't feel very responsive. Button presses while climbing/battling a colossus are treated by the game as less of a distinct command and as more of a polite request.... a request that is often blatantly ignored. While some of the time it's the game simply not responding to input by the player, oftentimes it's because your character is too busy flopping around on the colossus like a edematous skin tag to pay attention to anything you're asking him to do. \\"Please jump from the colossus's arm to his back.\\" \\"Sorry can't do that, as he's breathing heavily right now, and for some reason I can't stop seizing like an epileptic when the colossus is doing anything other than standing completely still.\\" Where this becomes really stupid is when a colossus gets stuck in an endless loop of shaking, essentially rendering you immobile (yet floppy) until your grip runs out, and you fall to the ground, only to begin the climbing process yet again. Considering that the vast bulk of the SOC game experience revolves around this kind of stuff, I quickly found myself getting frustrated, bored, and not having fun. And videogame are supposed to be fun, right? If you don't believe this, you're playing them for the wrong reason.   I'll stop there, as this is getting too long already. What makes SOC game so annoying is not the individual design problems in isolation, it's how they conspire against you all at once, and throughout the entire game. Seriously, if the controls and camera were improved, intuitive, and responsive, then I would've rated this game much higher. Both games, in fact. But just a few design flaws can ruin a game if they're frustrating enough. Life's too busy to waste this kind of time on poor/lazy game design.   **The music is incredible, by the way, and is some of the best I've experienced in the medium. For this reason, I bumped the overall rating from one star to two. Really, I should've just bought the official SOC soundtrack and saved myself the wasted time. Hindsight's 20/20.  -------  So that's my differing opinion on the ICO/SCO collection. Not that it's going to persuade anyone to avoid these games, but I find it's always refreshing to hear a viewpoint different from that of the majority. Do what you want with it.

B000003S5H|5.0|Moon Safari, the second album by the French band Air, is notable perhaps not for introducing groundbreaking technosonic techniques as much as imagining a world in which the palette of sonic textures were so rich with technosonic elements. The first track off the album is a long instrumental that conjures immediately the world in which the rest of the album will transpire: a retro-futurist 1960's, with an optimistic perspective on technology and yes, the acquisition of that final frontier, space. The opening strains of the surprisingly long (over 7 minutes) La Femme D'Argent imagine Brazilian lounge, setting solidly into the realm of breezy cool. It is only on the periphery we are reminded of the electronic tones, be it either the exaggerated crispness of violin overtones or the bubbling synth that lifts the groove seamlessly back to speed. As the track progresses though, the template of bass/drum groove becomes rarified, and what only appeared to be accents before swarm thickly together, constituting all of the song by the end.  Immediately following the opening tour of the album, Moon Safari drops into probably the most stand-alone track offered, Sexy Boy. The arresting quality of the bass hook comes not from the melody as much as the thick vibrato with which it is announced. While the vibrato fades from the bass, it never fully leaves the song, transitioning from instrument to instrument and giving the whole piece a hazy quality - wonderfully offset by icy keyboard chimes that pierce the envelope occasionally.   The contrast between the first two tracks is probably the album's most arresting swing; the rest of the time, Air is enjoying the futuro-lounge territory they have scoped out for themselves. Certain tracks borrow less from synths and retro organs less than others - in particular, You Make It Easy and Ce Matin La could have been products of tropicalia were it not for the occasional sparkle of synth accenting each. On other tracks however, Air is much more inclined to invoke modular synths and electronic organs, giving the texture a decidedly forward-looking sound. These songs - Kelly Watch The Stars, Talisman - gain much from their steady underlying beats, which work so well together with the organ and synth sound, affecting dispassion as best they can. The insistence of the rhythm tracks in both of these songs permits the melodic elements to lift almost impossibly high, affecting the crystalline tone we have come to know so well.

If you're read other reviews, you'll have seen that UV is NOT compatible with iTunes, but instead requires its own playback client (Flixster).  This review is about my experience with the process and service.  The movie comes with a card that contains the UV code and the web site information where to register.  The sign-up process took 5 minutes and was pretty simple.  To my surprise, I didn't just get access to the \\"Green Lantern\\" movie, but also to \\"Green Lantern: Emerald Knights\\".  I don't know if that was part of a promotion or if every copy comes with it, but either way, I was positively surprised by this.  I've used Flixster on the Kindle Fire and iPhone 4S.  It works great on both devices.  I have NOT tried using AirPlay on the iPhone to see if I could send the movie to the Apple TV.  My only annoyance at the moment is that the Flixster app shows ads at times when you start it up.  There are no ads during the movie, so it's not disruptive.  However, I would have preferred if they had a dedicated UV playback app instead of putting it into Flixster, which is also used for other services where ads can be justified.  In short, if you're holding back on buying this package, because of UV, don't worry about it.  I was lucky to pick up this movie during Black Friday sales for $9.99 and thought that even if UV doesn't work, I'll still have the DVD and Bluray as fallback.  I'm glad I did and I would not hesitate to buy other UV movies in the future.  That said, if given a choice, I would opt for the old Digital Copy via iTunes since I'm more familiar with it.  Hope this helps!

1451627289|5.0|Wow!  A Stephen King novel that seemed so intimidating at 849 pages has now been devoured and digested and I can safely say that \\"11/22/63\\" was a remarkable achievement.  Like so many others, I have been a Constant Reader of everything King has published over the past 35 plus years, and I definitly found \\"11/22/63\\" to be an upgrade from a great deal of his recent material. In many ways, it was a return to form for King while taking on the very tricky and fictional mine field of time travel.  Many lament that Stephen King has never adhered to the \\"less is more\\" concept and sometimes they have been right.  Yet in the case of \\"11/22/63\\", the extended form of the novel is less due to bloat (there surely is some superfluous material crying for editing) than due to the subject matter and storyline.  When Jake Eppling becomes George Amberson and steps through the portal in Al's Diner to return to 1958 to attempt to save JFK from being assassinated in 1963, he is forced to \\"live\\" for 5 years in a time and place unknown and totally foreign to him.  Focusing most of the narrative on how he adjusts (or doesn't, in some cases) over those five years provides the reader with the real story here--Jake is a man out of time (like a fish out of water) devoted to a seemingly impossible task who falls in love with a very special woman who was living 53 years before his time, causing Jake to be ultimately confronted with challenges and choices that should make for provocative thinking for any reader.  This long section of the book does several things including, conveying the love story of star-crossed (time-crossed lovers), developing a sense of time and place, and providing a needed and satisfying development of the prominent characters in the book.  Certainly the story would have been much thinner were it not for the tragic love story of Jake (George) and Sadie.   Having lived through that era, as did King, I enjoyed his reflections on the music, movies, and culture of the time--certainly a simpler time (no internet, no cell phones, no computers), yet certainly a challenging time (racism, threat of WWIII).  And King's handling of the whole time travel paradox is well conceived compared to many of those time travel novels that fail under their own pretensions.  Can Jake change the course of history by going back in time?  He quickly discovers that the past is obdurate--the past fights to prevent change.  If he can change the past, should he?  Are there consequences to any change--oh yes, Constant Reader--the \\"butterfly effect\\" insures there will be consequences of some manner.  Much of the joy of this novel, beyond the love story, the depiction of the late 50's early 60's era, and the question of whether he can succeed in his mission, is the provocative questions King's narrative poses for his readers.  Would we readily accept the opportunity to step through a portal in time; indeed, if we did, would we be willing to live 5 years in a different time and place in an attempt to change the course of history (not knowing if we successfully could)?  Would we be open to truly living in that time to the extent of falling in love, or marrying, or having children or whatever?  And how would we handle the so called \\"butterfly effect\\" that posits that anything changed in the past, no matter how small, has consequences in the future?  I found \\"11/22/63\\" to be a thoughtful attempt at the time travel theme by Stephen King that provided the reader with a genuine love story wrapped in paradoxes and enigmas surrounding time, timeless love, and the enduring curiosity of man.

1451627289|5.0|When Stephen King is on his game it's like watching Tiger Woods when he was in his prime.  There is simply no one, past or present, who does what he does better.  I'm happy to report that after fifteen years of frustratingly uneven work which demonstrated only brief flashes of his early career brilliance, 11/22/63 is the novel which resoundingly reasserts King's place as Grand Master of the art.  This is his finest work of fiction since The Green Mile.    Like Kings previous novels, The Dead Zone and The Green Mile, 11/22/63 centers around a protagonist called upon to do extraordinary things in the midst of leading an ordinary, unremarkable life.  In the case of 11/22/63, the events center around the assasination of John F. Kennedy and the efforts of the protagonist to alter this watershed moment in United States history.    11/22/63 is a genre mash up which in the hands of a lesser author (or even the Stephen King of five years ago) might have quikly turned absurd and unbelievable. However, 11/22/63 turns out to be an expertly woven tale which incorporates elements from science fiction, alternate history fiction and romance and even manages to throw in a side order of 1940's style road film.   11/22/63 gets five stars from me, but that's not to say it is without flaw.  Like most of King's work of late, it's a bit longer than neccessary.  There are also occasional descriptions of events in the book which drag on too long and cause the story to briefly bog down.  It's hard to care much about this novels minor flaws though when the characters come to life so brilliantly and the over all story is so original and down right riveting.  Put 11/22/63 on your shopping list.  You'll be glad you did.

0307593312|5.0|At 925 pages, 1Q84 is a very long novel that doesn't feel long.  Both because it covers less than a year and because the main characters, Tengo and Aomame don't evolve.  They become clearer about what matters to them but they don't develop in the way you would expect of characters in a long novel. It is significant that Aomame is reading Proust because time is fluid and deceptive in 1Q84 both because of how the action unfolds but also in the reading of the book.  On one hand I knew it took a long time to read but on the other hand time seems to have disappeared.<br /><br />A synopsis of this book would read as follows: Aomame a part-time assassin and full-time masseuse enters slightly alternative world and is on a mission to kill leader of a sinister cult. Still in love with a boy she briefly held hands with in elementary school she searching for him to re-kindle the love affair.  Tengo, a math teacher and writer, re-writes a fable-like novel that becomes a huge bestseller and leads him to be threatened by the same cult whose leader Aomame is to kill.  Tengo is also in love with Aomame and realizes as well that he must find her.<br /><br />Only a writer of incredible power could take this material and transform it into an unforgettable tale of mystery, magic and romance.  Despite being translated from Japanese into English, it still captures that other worldly, trance-like quality.  I am stumped at how Murakami is able to achieve this.<br /><br />The book is more than merely a strangely readable story.  It is a meditation on the mystery of love, a demonstration of how the creative process works, and even of the difficulty in holding on to judgments about facts when they suddenly shift in shape and meaning.  The characters are also strangely compelling.  Many facts about them get repeated again and again yet in such a way as to give these qualities a hypnotic effect on the reader.  For example, we are repeatedly told about one of the major character's misshapen head and after awhile that quality takes on a wide range of meanings and makes that character both deeply sympathetic and disgusting at the same time.<br /><br />This is not my favorite Murakami novel.  That would \\"Kafka on the Shore\\" which was so rich and evocative in way 1Q84 is not.  Still, this is one of handful of mind-altering novels I've read in the last decade.takes on a wide range of meanings and makes that character both deeply sympathetic and disgusting at the same time. B004EI3ON4|5.0|The soundtrack to Tron: Legacy is a brilliant achievement for electronic music duo Daft Punk. The duo, known for their house and technopop productions, collaborated with music supervisor Jason Bentley and orchestrator Joseph Trapanese to create a score that merged orchestral music with electronic music. Their product is a brilliant soundtrack that marks a significant place in electronic music history.    The idea of merging orchestral music and electronic music has been popular for many years, but Tron: Legacy is by far one of the most successful attempts at this endeavor. For starters, the soundtrack is entertaining. It is an intense and enjoyable listen. Secondly, the score truly captures the tone and essence of the film. The piece mirrors the intensity, drama, and suspense expressed throughout the film. The movie takes place primarily in a futuristic, digital world. The piece sounds out-of-this-world and original without sounding like the cliché computer sounding music heard in many movies that surround around a futuristic or technology heavy theme.   In addition to creating a good soundtrack, Daft Punk's achievements with Tron: Legacy include their seamless fusion of electronic music with Trapanese's orchestral score. Rather than sounding choppy, the two different styles of music compliment each other. In \\"Solar Sailer,\\" synthesized tones and sequencers can be heard while a chorus of violins beautifully fills the background.   \\"The Game Has Changed\\" serves as another example of a great mixture of orchestral music and electronic music. Sequencers and granulized sound come in and out to compliment the music of the orchestra that's playing the meat of the song. At times, the orchestral music is distorted by technological means, adding a sense of suspense and to the song and continuing the digital theme present throughout the soundtrack and the film.  Throughout a listen of the entire soundtrack, one can hear everything from orchestral horns and violins to synthesized pianos and other computer sounds that can't truly be described by relation to a real instrument. Overall, the soundtrack is more orchestral music with subtle electronic lines in it, a fact that seems to displease many fans.   Daft Punk has been criticized for Tron: Legacy. Many fans miss Daft Punks old style (which can appropriately be described as creating new style) and accuse the duo of \\"selling out.\\" Listeners need to remember that this soundtrack is meant to be just that, a soundtrack. The score to a soundtrack of a film is not going to be the same as the music on an album that is meant to be listened to independently. A film's soundtrack is not going to sound like Daft Punk's famed Discover album. Daft Punk made a piece that follows the style of a film score because it is a film score and must operate in that capacity. With that being said, the piece is still quite original and their willingness to so close integrate electronic music with orchestral music is quite progressive and far from \\"selling out.\\"  Individuals like Iannis Xenakis, Robert Moog, and Max Mathews would be delighted to see the essential role that electronic music made in Tron: Legacy. Involvement of computer music in film has travelled a long way from \\"Daisy Bell\\" in 2001: A Space Odyssey and the trautonium in Alfred Hitchcock's The Birds. Integrating synthesizers, sequencers, modulators, and other technological instruments with classical orchestral performers is no easy tasks, but Daft Punk executed it successfully. The duo should be applauded for their progressive, daring, and successful efforts.

030726839X|5.0|This author has been on somewhat of tear as of late, this his third World War II book published in the last seven years, (Armageddon and Retribution the other two), and a fourth volume just out. Winston's War also adds to the recent spate of books - Carlo D'Este's Warlord; Andrew Robert's Hitler and Churchill and Masters and Commanders, and Berthon and Potts', Warlords - \\"analyzing\\" Churchill's leadership role and his success/failure as Great Britain's Commander in Chief. Hastings' new book differs from these previous Churchill book specifically in its focus - this book is all Churchill and all WWII, and is another excellent addition to the history of both.<br /><br />First a brief note on Hastings' \\"writing style\\". The author has seemingly read everything available - first-hand accounts from diaries and letters, newspaper accounts, military archives and records, etc. - and combined all this research/data into a very compelling, insightful, extremely well written account. The author is also anything but subtle. In this book for instance, Henry \\"Chips\\" Cannon is described as the \\"consummate ass\\" and that Lord Gort, although personally courageous - \\"lacked brains\\" - this within the first 25 pages. The point here is that Hastings does not mince words and is never shy about voicing his educated opinion - including about Churchill.<br /><br />Winston's War picks up on May 10th, 1940, as Churchill assumed the premiership - \\"The Phony War\\" in Europe coming to an end with the Nazi Army mobilizing. Thus begins Churchill's heroic struggle to save his country and the world from Hitler's tyranny - and what a story it is. Hastings chronicles the dark yet heroic days of 1940-41 when Churchill and his countrymen stood alone; the partnership with Stalin and FDR after Barbarossa and Pearl Harbor; and as odd man out of The Big Three as events, Soviet man-power and American war production passed Churchill and his country by. And as Hastings painstakingly points out there was plenty ofChurchillian good and bad - decisions and behavior - during these war years, for Winston packed a half dozen lifetimes worth of activity in these busy years, which in and of itself was all part of a very busy and active life.<br /><br />This book is by no means a hatchet-job or even to a lesser degree - revisionist history. The author makes it very clear that the same qualities that propelled Churchill through the crisis of the early years of WWII are the same that frustrated his military men and his allies throughout the war's later years - Churchill was always Churchill - and the world is a better place for that.<br /><br />There is a treasure trove of information here not to be missed - An excellent book and highly recommended.

0765350386|4.0|When giving praise to Brandon Sanderson, Patrick Rothfuss (author of the King Killer Chronicles) once said that Brandon clearly missed the job working for big name comic books. After reading a wonderfully refreshing book like Mistborn, you can see why. As far as super powers go (and I use the term loosely here) the abilities of the Allomancers rivals that of our richest super hero mythologies, and as far as magic systems go, it rivals that of Robert Jordan's the One Power in the Wheel of Time series by way of sure ingeniousness alone. It's just that brilliant. This story is dying to be made into a comic book series (if you are in the comic book industry, PLEASE PLEASE give Mr. Sanderson a call!). After a stint of reading lesser fantasy books, I heard of Robin Hobb's praise of Brandon Sanderson and immediately familiarized myself with his body of work. And while I usually read an author's first break out novel before anything else, I decided to skip Elantris for now and read Brandon's best known work, the Mistborn trilogy. As it turned out, this will be the series that has Brandon up there with the Martins, Jordans, and Eriksons of the heavyweight fantasy hall of fame. I often say in my reviews that I'm not a HATE IT 1 star reviewer, or LOVE IT 5 star reviewer, but I must admit, this is really pushing the LOVE IT reviewer in me. I give it 4.5 out of 5, and here's why.  The front Mistborn page on Amazon summarizes the story, so I'll explain why I wholly recommend this novel.    Pros:  The magic system (Allomancers Assemble!)- Okay, so I've already talked about this, but give me a moment to gush here. If your looking for a new and refreshing magic system, look no further. The idea of Allomancers and Mistborn burning metals to gain super human abilities is highly remarkable, I can't stress this enough. The beauty of it is its uncanny ability to be both strange and very natural at the same time. Brandon Sanderson describes in an interview that it comes from the idea ofhumans burning our foods into sugars then proteins that allows us to move throughout the day. The uncanny part is the consumption of metal, but it's written and executed in such a human way that you'll often stop and wonder to yourself (if I take a bite of my quarter could I fly off rooftops too?!)--Believe me when I say, 'no, you won't.' But, you will honestly believe you can after reading about Kelsier and Vin doing it.  The World of Mistborn (Give us a world worth fighting for)- The great part of Brandon Sanderson's writing is his masterful hold on world building. After reading the prologue alone, it will seem as if you're walking the ash filled streets of Luthadel, looking up at a blood red sun in the sky, watching the soot fall like black snow. His attention to detail is superb, and it fills you with this sense that you really would like to see things change for the better. Its almost as if Brandon builds a sympathy for the world itself, as it seems as much enslaved as many of the skaa people that inhabit it. The rituals of the nobles are also fully realized, and the small thing that I loved the most were the religions that Sazed knew about it.   The characters (A world is only as interesting as those inhabiting it)- I might get some flak here, but I didn't find the characters to be cookie cutter at all, like many other reviewers. Vin and Kelsier are both fully fleshed out, but it's because they are the focus of the story. I loved everything about Kelsier from the very beginning. He's humble yet brazen at the same time, he's courageous, and actually really funny, something you don't see very much in fantasy. Vin is vulnerable and you feel bad for her, but I didn't particularly like her at the beginning. But this was intended, she needed to learn how to trust, and when she begins to, she gains your sympathy by the reader wanting to be in her shoes, flying over building tops and hollering in joy. I feel like Breeze, Ham, and the others were supposed to be flat in this book, but just because they're flat doesn't make them 'cookie cutter'. They are accents in the story, they move things along, but they are rendered well enough to be entities with their own weight. I also disagree with what people say of the dialogue. I didn't feel it too self explanatory. Better to understand what's going on than to be confused. It's just fine.  The con(s):  The plot (Atium's 11)- There is really only one gripe I had in this novel, and its plot related. I felt the story had a lot of build up, but largely did nothing with it (and there is one particular part where the suspense does build--when the crap hits the fan--but then falls flat) not until the end at least, which had me throwing the book in nervousness. I have heard many times that this was a heist novel, but oddly enough, there was very little heisting to be had. I liked Vin's excursions into the nobility, but I wanted to see what the others were up to. The only time we do see what Breeze, Ham and the others are doing, they only train Vin with her powers. The heist part had to do with the 11th metal (Ocean's 11 reference? Maybe) and I really enjoyed the mystery behind it. Halfway through the novel turned into something else, but I was willing to forgive it because this is the first book.  And that's the brilliant part. This book covers in one novel what many fantasy stories take 5 books to complete. It takes the peasant boy and dark lord cliche and flips it on its head, that's the real beauty of Mistborn. Overall, read this book, read it as quickly as you can. I guarantee you, you won't regret it.

B000OEZ88G|4.0|Why does the book contain studies and reviews of almost exclusively U.S. companies?  Perhaps the framework is flawed because it does not apply to other cultures such as Asia, or Europe.  Maybe Asian and European companies did not make the top list of visionary companies.  The authors only touched on it in a short paragraph.  I feel that in today's flattened and globalized world, these concerns should be addressed more concretely.  This way, the framework would be much stronger after knowing the reasons of why the list of companies is mainly US companies.  Perhaps an additional chapter or two to elaborate on the notion that the framework of visionary companies is not cultural bound would be more valuable.   It seems that the authors avoid the context of culture. For example, on the dimension of uncertainty avoidance, a study found that \\"France and Russia were high; Hong Kong and the United States were low\\".  Collins and Porras would not contend that values are different between cultures or between companies of different cultures.  However, they would contend that visionary companies, whether in Asia or in Europe or anywhere, must uphold their core ideology no matter what.  However, it is apparent that values are different across cultures, whether it's Asia or Europe.  What would be interesting is an empirical study to see how the author's framework and the surrounding ideas of visionary companies apply to other cultures.  For example, how would the structure for preserving the core ideology such as commitments to BHAGS, promoting from within, creating a cult-like culture and maximizing profits fair in other parts of the world?    Although the book's ideas and analysis revolves around the corporate world, it is interesting that it could be applied to an individual as it resonates on a personal level.  Core ideologies form the fundamental values for the company's sense of purpose or their reasons for existence.  Similarly, individuals have fundamental values and a sense of purpose for existence that works very much the same way as described in the framework of visionary companies.  As individuals, everyone have a set of core beliefs of what is right and what is wrong, and how one should act and behave in ways that are ideal.  For example, one may live by the guidelines of righteousness, charitable, and respect. The authors noted that visionary companies could sacrifice everything, even if it means losing money, which is the bloodline for their survival, if it means preserving the core values.  On an individual level, it means that one needs to find what is meaningful to them and commit to it completely.  This commitment will ultimately bring success.  Collins and Porras have cleared up an often vague and fuzzy concept of \\"vision.\\"  As the authors say, the idea of vision \\"conjures up all kinds of images\\" (219.)  Many often confuse a company's vision with its mission.  Although related, the two ideas are different.    The authors have put together a coherent framework of researched case studies that make for instructive reading by \\"pointing out telling trends\\" and giving readers \\"some delightful old dirt on the companies' early struggles\\" .  Moreover, unlike many academic books, which are more theory based, \\"Built to Last\\" is more practical.  It provides guidelines, like a handbook that teaches the principles of what it takes to be \\"visionary.\\"  The book has been in existence for 12 years and continues to sell by the truckload.  It is easy to see why.  Many books seem to have the answer until a few years go by and it no longer fits.  \\"Built to Last\\" is a classic and its principles are timeless.  As the authors indicated, many of today's management concepts have been built off these principles.  Often time, people forget the fundamentals.  This book brings people back to reality and keeps people in touch with the core foundation of success.  It inspires people because it give them hope and a vision that could guide them to be \\"Builtto Last.\\"

0385534639|4.0|I finished this debut novel at 2 am. Instead of sleeping. When I had a flight leaving at 6 am. I could end the review right there, but I think it deserves a little more explanation.<br /><br />First line: \\"The circus arrives without warning. No announcements precede it, no paper notices on downtown posts and billboards, no mentions or advertisements in local newspapers. It is simply there, when yesterday it was not.\\" Talk about setting a mood! At first I thought \\"huh, interesting.\\" but felt no real investment. As I wandered through bits of the circus it became so immersive that by the end I thought this opening line completely brilliant. It grows on you, seeps into your soul and stays there. As a first line, it's enough of a hook to make you read on to the next page. As a synopsis of the whole story, it's fantastic.<br /><br />Mechanics: My biggest issue with this story is that it's in third person, present tense. I realize this is the fashion and fad these days for literary novels, but I hate it. It makes everything distant and cold. It makes me feel as though I'm looking through a glass window at the action. A dirty glass window. A dirty glass window with the words \\"wash me\\" written in the dirt. That said, I think parts of it simply had to be this way. There are two narratives going on here...one is meant to describe what you, the reader, are doing as you make your way through the circus. That has to be third person, present tense. The rest is the backstory of the circus itself. To me, that part would have been more intimate told in the normal past tense. I found my head twisting the present tense into past tense as I read which threw me out of the story. However, the story itself is fascinating. So gripping, in fact, that I pressed onward despite the mechanics. The tense is easy to forget when people are talking, and since there is more dialog in the second half of the story that might be why some think the story picks up then. It's not the story, it's the mechanics. No matter how much in fashion it is, it's simply not comfortable to read for any length of time for the average person. This is not a script...and shouldn't read like one. There, done ranting. Don't let this stop you from reading the book because truly the story more than makes up for my little pet peeve.<br /><br />Voice: I found the voice to be haunting and captivating. The sentence structure is simple and straightforward. It sucks you in much the way I imagine the circus would if you were to stumble upon it.<br /><br />Plot: This is a literary novel, not a thriller. It's not action packed, but it is STORY packed. By that I mean the entire story is like entering the circus it describes. You cautiously inch your way forward, discovering more and more as the story unfolds until you are up at 2 am trying to get to the end and hoping against hope that it can wrap up this enchanting tale in a way that won't leave you wanting. Well done, Ms. Morgenstern, well done.<br /><br />Characters: I have to admit, due to the glass lens of present tense, the characters all feel a bit distant. I'm not sure I cared so much about them as I did about the circus itself. I think this might even be exactly what the author intends. Even one of the main characters spends her time finding a way for the circus to sustain itself without her. The same could be said for the story. I found this fascinating. I don't think I've ever read a story before where I didn't really care about the characters at all and yet was so captivated to see it unfold. I'm still not sure what to make of that and how it was done. I suspect I'll be coming back to this story to read it again when I've let it sit. The real main character through the entire story is the circus. I have to admit, I'm in love with the circus and now have an intense longing to decorate in black and white.<br /><br />The Night Circus haunts me. I find myself thinking of it at odd times, like the reveurs in the story. Wishing the story ittells is a true story. Hoping it's real. Needing it to be real. If it grabs you like it did me, then perhaps you'll join me. Put on a black coat and a red scarf and come on out. I'll be wandering around outside the city in the evenings, looking for the circus.al. If it grabs you like it did me, then perhaps you'll join me. Put on a black coat and a red scarf and come on out. I'll be wandering around outside the city in the evenings, looking for the circus.

B005HIBWFW|4.0|After literally decades of waiting, it is a joy to at last have this remarkable and grievously neglected masterwork available on DVD. The good news is that it's a clean and complete print with fine image and sound. The crying shame is how cavalierly it has been packaged. The manufacturers simply took no pains whatever. Inconceivably, there is no discrete chaptering. One starts the movie playing and is able to advance or reverse through it at 10 minute intervals. That is all. Well, aside from having the original theatrical trailer included which, under the circumstances, feels like a bone thrown to a dog. Looking past this unforgivable disregard for the work, however, it remains one of the great masterpieces of the early 1970s. It is a must-have for any serious film buff.<br /><br />It's especially important to cut through the totally false reputation with which it was saddled by vicious critics at the time of its release. First among these was Vincent Canby of the New York Times, who accused it of being a fantasy that completely falsified Tchaikovsky's life. One can only marvel at the arrogance of someone so respected making a charge which is so mind-bogglingly false. Any look at the facts reveals that Russell and his co-writer Melvyn Bragg stick closely to recorded history, only dovetailing certain events and combining certain characters to achieve well-paced dramatic action. They add absolutely nothing to the narrative which isn't either a direct event from the composer's life or which combines aspects of characters or personality traits into bundles that most powerfully communicate the underlying truths surrounding his tempestuous life.<br /><br />Among its prime virtues are its incandescent performances. Richard Chamberlain captures the gentility of his character while masterfully navigating its frequent hysteria, along with giving one of the most credible on-screen representations of piano virtuosity. Glenda Jackson, alternately ingratiating, steely, pathetic, and finally insane, is a tower of creative fire. They're beautifully supported by Christopher Gable's wonderfully effeminate Russian count, Kenneth Colley's scheming brother, Max Adrian's pompous impresario, Izabella Telezynska's cunning benefactress, and Maureen Pryor's venal matriarch.<br /><br />Best of all, though, is Russell's almost preternatural feel for the music itself, every note of it by Tchaikovsky. From the opening, a little-known scherzo whose jarring shift from major to minor underlines the film's title, to the notorious 1812 Overture sequence where the lead characters are decapitated by canon fire, we are treated to a marriage of sight and sound unequalled in all cinema. No film before or since has ever approached the brilliance of Russell's musical visualizations, the standout being the first Piano Concerto's second movement, given in full and tying together all the film's conceptual and emotional threads in perfect synchronization with Tchaikovsky's music.<br /><br />Perhaps one day the DVD makers will deign to give this wonderful film the respect it is due. But for now, the fine print alone is more than worth the price.is a tower of creative fire. They're beautifully supported by Christopher Gable's wonderfully effeminate Russian count, Kenneth Colley's scheming brother, Max Adrian's pompous impresario, Izabella Telezynska's cunning benefactress, and Maureen Pryor's venal matriarch.  Best of all, though, is Russell's almost preternatural feel for the music itself, every note of it by Tchaikovsky. From the opening, a little-known scherzo whose jarring shift from major to minor underlines the film's title, to the notorious 1812 Overture sequence where the lead characters are decapitated by canon fire, we are treated to a marriage of sight and sound unequalled in all cinema. No film before or since has ever approached the brilliance of Russell's musical visualizations, the standout being the first Piano Concerto's second movement, given in full and tying together all the film's conceptual and emotional threads in perfect synchronization with Tchaikovsky's music. 0446573019|5.0|I really liked this book. Both John Puller in Zero Day and Jack Reacher in the Reacher series come from a military family, are military cops who are very tall and strong, very knowledgeable about both military and practical matters, very tenacious, very ethically minded, independent, highly respected by high-level military personnel, have one brother who was in the military, and generally work alone on complicated cases. Even their names are similar: Jack and John, Reacher and Puller; one reaches and the other pulls, and both know what they want and get it, quickly and completely. And both prefer that people call them by their surname. Reacher, of course, has now left the military because he handled a case in an ethical manner. Puller faces the same problem in this book.<br /><br /> Puller's superior officer gives him an assignment that he says is very sensitive and that very high government officials are watching. But, strangely, he tells Puller that he must handle it all by himself. A Lieutenant Colonel, his wife and two kids are killed in a small West Virginia coal town. He tells Puller to find out who did it and why. But Puller is unable to understand why, if this case is so sensitive and the highest levels are following it, he must handle it alone. And why is he, a highly decorated and competent soldier, assigned a run of the mill murder case? And aren't the local cops able to handle it? Is he being set up? Unlike Reacher who travels with only a tooth brush, Puller packs his car with everything, from a machine gun and other weapons to bio-hazard suits. He soon discovers four more deaths, then others. The novel begins strong and continues strong throughout. The characters are finely drawn. It is unusually suspenseful and good.

B004EI3ON4|5.0|Where to begin? Admittedly, this soundtrack is intricately composed - beyond my limited musical understanding; however, I like to think that I possess a keen ear for quality music and a passionate appreciation for film, so that will have to suffice in my recommendation of this album.<br /><br />The well-established French electronic music duo known as Daft Punk has captured the hearts and ears of electronic music enthusiasts since the early 90s. As the main composers of the Tron Legacy soundtrack, the duo set aside a couple years of their career to concentrate on mastering this album, which features a 100-piece orchestra from London. With that being said, it was a no brainer for me to take an hour out of my day to pleasure my ears with this brilliant musical score. The soundtrack certainly goes against Daft Punk's traditional style of composition, fleeing from their typical house music feel to a more theatrical, synth-heavy approach accompanied by the talented orchestra.<br /><br />My personal experience with Tron: Legacy began about a month before the highly anticipated film hit theaters when I stumbled across a \\"leak\\" of the soundtrack online. With Daft Punk as the featured composer of the soundtrack, I knew I could not go wrong with giving it a listen. Right off the bat, the classical music approach is presented by \\"Overture\\" and then \\"The Grid,\\" both of which sooth the listener over with epically long build-ups. In \\"The Grid,\\" one of the only tracks on the albums that has vocals, Daft Punk uses a powerful quote by Flynn (\\"And then, one day...I got in\\") to maintain its slow and deliberate introduction of the plot. After the two introductory songs end, Daft Punk hits you with a trippy, fast-paced motorcycle ride through the city with Flynn's son, the protagonist of the film, with the song that is conveniently called \\"The Son of Flynn.\\" In theaters, this was the first scene where the audience really experienced the full effect of the 3D experience so it really got my adrenaline pumping. The beginning of the song is probably my favorite beat of the entire soundtrack.<br /><br />After a few tracks into the album, I began to notice how well Daft Punk managed to transition from song to song. From gradual to being caught off guard by abrupt endings, the album flows smoothly and always delivers a theatrical feel, no matter speed at which it makes your head bob. The pauses between tracks really enhance the mood of the soundtrack, really sending you on an up and down roller coaster ride of emotion as the tension gradually builds. We finally reach the climax of the film during \\"Flynn Lives,\\" which is unbelievably well-composed.<br /><br />\\"TRON Legacy\\" was a typical song to be playing during the credits scene at the end. As someone who places high importance on having a quality finale song, I was not impressed; I did not even really enjoy the tune. With that being said, this was the main flaw I could pick out with the soundtrack.<br /><br />Overall, I thought Daft Punk did a brilliant job composing an original score that ultimately enhanced my complete experience with Tron Legacy. I would strongly recommend listening to the album before watching the film; doing so will give you time to enjoy a masterful technosonic adventure unfold through your headphones before you are able to match the exciting visual experience with the sounds. You know how people always say, \\"the book was sooo much better than the movie?\\" I guess that is what I am trying to say about this soundtrack in comparison to the film. I say check it out for sure.g. The beginning of the song is probably my favorite beat of the entire soundtrack.  After a few tracks into the album, I began to notice how well Daft Punk managed to transition from song to song. From gradual to being caught off guard by abrupt endings, the album flows smoothly and always delivers a theatrical feel, no matter speed at which it makes your head bob. The pauses between tracks really enhance the mood of the soundtrack, really sending you on an up and down roller coaster ride of emotion as the tension gradually builds. We finally reach the climax of the film during \\"Flynn Lives,\\" which is unbelievably well-composed.  \\"TRON Legacy\\" was a typical song to be playing during the credits scene at the end. As someone who places high importance on having a quality finale song, I was not impressed; I did not even really enjoy the tune. With that being said, this was the main flaw I could pick out with the soundtrack.  Overall, I thought Daft Punk did a brilliant job composing an original score that ultimately enhanced my complete experience with Tron Legacy. I would strongly recommend listening to the album before watching the film; doing so will give you time to enjoy a masterful technosonic adventure unfold through your headphones before you are able to match the exciting visual experience with the sounds. You know how people always say, \\"the book was sooo much better than the movie?\\" I guess that is what I am trying to say about this soundtrack in comparison to the film. I say check it out for sure.

B000003S5H|5.0|As the name suggests, listening to Air's Moon Safari does not seem to be about a world that we're familiar with. By the first track, you are strapped into your aural rocket ship and allowing the gravity to slip away from you. You fly beyond the atmosphere into a different world far away.  Each track of the wonderfully rich album is a new experience like visiting a new planet; it is exotic and foreign yet comforting in its tranquil nature. We're not scared to go beyond what we know. In fact, by the end of the album, we crave more. In a dreamlike state, the mix of voice, synthesizer, and real instruments is a lovely amalgam of the known and the unknown, the tactile and the intangible, the present and the future. At length, the album manages to remain both serene and interesting in its variety of textures, instruments, and timbres, not an easy feat in collection featuring mainly slow tempos with subtle melodies. It is through the incredible development and variety of textures and timbres achieved that gives Moon Safari its rich fabric of sound and its alien elegance.   Moon Safari was Air's, a duo featuring French musicians Nicolas Godin and Jean-Benoit Dunckel, first album released in 1998. Incredibly well-received, it quickly became the standard of electronic chill-out music, and no other album has been considered as skillful or interesting as it. Its popularity and legacy was so great that the album was rereleased ten years later in 2008 with an extra disc. This was my first time listening to the original album all the way through and it is not hard to see the appeal. While soothing and elegant in its tranquility, it does not lack at all in interesting and innovative music that is constantly morphing and manipulating into something unexpected and new. The music is incredibly effective, creating more like an entire environment and an all-encompassing sound experience instead of just aural pieces. Air's music is not especially dynamic in mood or volume, but it is absolutely not to be mistaken for boring and static.   Many of the tracks of Moon Safari begin with an unrecognizable ambience that melts into the dominant rhythm and melody of the piece. The rhythms and melodies patiently emerge, and are constantly developing one they arrive. Using an array of instruments including but not limited to synthesizers, percussion, bass, keyboards, and the human voice. Some of the instrumental tracks lean towards a bluesy and funky feel with electronic infusion, such as \\"La Femme D'Argent.\\" Other tracks, especially ones featuring the human voice, like \\"Sexy Boy\\" and \\"All I Need,\\" are more sultry and sensual in their sound and are intriguing juxtapositions of voice and electronic ambience. Air implements the human voice in other ways by electronically processing it into a nearly unrecognizable musical element that fades in and out of the other electronic instruments of the song. This can be seen especially in \\"Remember.\\"  In my opinion, it is the later tracks on the album that are especially enchanting. I feel that these tracks use a particularly rich variety of textures and really evoke distinct moods, especially the last two tracks \\"Ce Matin La\\" and \\"New Star in the Sky,\\" which are both strikingly emotive and varied.   Listening to Moon Safari gives one the feeling that they are floating by a strange and unfamiliar world. The music takes many qualities from our own reality, like the human voice and familiar instruments, but Air has tweaked and twisted these qualities into something that does not quite resemble what we know here on Earth. The resulting sounds create a world strange to us, but also tranquil and soothing in nature. We release ourselves from our reality and let Air guide us through the album, showing us their new world. It is almost a passive activity listening to this album:  all you have to do is sit back, relax, and enjoy the beautiful and exotic voyage that Air takes you on.
