Furthermore, imo, it's just wrong that this schlop is being added into the vaulted Lynyrd Skynyrd canon.  'Pink isn't well, he stayed back at the hotel.'
B000002GVO|5.0|It's funny... I swear I should have been born in another decade other than the nineties, which, in my opinion, contained the worst music through the decades.  Just this past summer, while perusing through a glorious flea market, I came upon an album.  Mint condition it was in, and for just five dollars, I considered it a steal.  Hotel California (1976) by the Eagles is perhaps one of the most influential albums of all time.  A small local band they began as, but their influence on music rocked generations.  I only regret that I wasn't there to experience it firsthand.  Yet reliving the magic this way is part of the joy that I get from vinyl record shopping.  It is very difficult for me to listen to CDs now, after hearing the unique sounds of an album on vinyl.  For the most part, all of my favorite albums were originally released on vinyl and then re-released on disc form.  There is just something about placing the needle, listening to the slight, natural scratches, and then flipping it over to begin the magic all over again.   But I digress.   Taking it home to play on my record player, I feel excited.  I had never heard many of the songs, even though they had been recorded over thirty years before.  Unlike all of my other records, there isn't a song that I do not like on this album.  It is rare nowadays to find such a gem, especially when you can simply buy only the songs that you like, instead of having to buy the entire vinyl or CD.    As the record begins to spin faster, I admire the album cover while waiting for the music to begin.  Covers used to be somewhat of a competition with other bands.  There were rarely any other sources of pictures for the music, and often times, the cover was the artistic representation of what the album was supposedly about.  While this cover isn't as spectacular as say Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band, it is very reminiscent of the overall music: mysterious and exotic.  I have to admit that this is something that I miss aboutmusic these days: the artwork.  With iTunes, all you purchase is the song, not the artwork that comes with the music.  When I rifle through records, I specifically look at the cover, and even if I don't necessarily know the artist, if the cover artwork is beautiful, it is worth the money.  Finally, the first haunting sounds of \\"Hotel California\\" caress my ears and instantly relax me.  Perhaps one of the most recognizable songs in music, I immediately respect it for what it is: an instant and timeless classic, chockfull of hidden literary devices.  Don Henley's unbelievable voice captures you and pulls your mind into this Californian world he creates, filled with materialism and overindulgence.  The pessimism combined with surrealism throughout the song creates an incredibly eerie mood; ultimately, it combats the idea of an American Dream and presents a darker image of the paradise that America was thought to be, and not just California.  Concluding the song is some of the best guitar work in music history.  Don Felder and Joe Walsh battle it together out in a mind blowing, yet somehow note-perfect way, for almost two minutes.  Certainly a fantastic song and only a sneak peak at what is to come!  \\"New Kid in Town\\" may be completely different than \\"Hotel\\" in terms of subject matter, but it maintains that timeless feel to it.  The phenomenal harmonies compliment Glenn Frey's silky smooth voice to really illustrate a hopeless romantic feel.  Clocking in at just over five minutes, it is definitely one of my personal favorites of the Eagles.  Despite the country-esque feeling to it, the lyrics and backup vocals make this song a perfect one to turn up the dial on your speakers (or turntable) and lament over a lost love.    However, who thinks that the Eagles are a country should just listen again to the raucous electric guitar riff that starts off \\"Life in the Fast Lane.\\"  Much more edgy than any other song on the album and keeping the overall theme of excessive materialism, it is a great one to blast and will \\"surely make you lose your mind.\\"  A forgettable song, but \\"Fast Lane\\" is still a vital one to have on the album.  Transitioning strangely, the Eagles continue their pattern of a mellower song after a brash one.  \\"Wasted Time\\" is a lyrical ballad with beautiful electric guitar parts intermingled before and after the chorus.  The tortured vocals emanate from Henley and really reflect the lyrics.  Again, the Eagles have some of the best harmonic chord progressions there are, both vocally and instrumentally, and it is plainly evident on \\"Wasted Time.\\"  You won't want to miss this one, which closes the first side of the album in a very ethereal and otherworldly way.  Flipping the album over, I cringe at the thought of a reprise.  Honestly, I hate them.  I just listened to the song once already, and although I understand that it helps to transition into the next tune, it just doesn't seem necessary.  But this one is necessary.  On the contrary, \\"Wasted Time (Reprise)\\" is a must for this album.  I suppose that is one good thing about CDs; the songs flow into each other without the interruption of having to turn the record over and break the overall musicality of the songs.  However, the classic sounds of a string orchestra pulls the listener seamlessly right back into the world of Hotel California.  And at just over a minute, it doesn't distract and most importantly leads right into the next great tune.  \\"Victim of Love\\" is a fun, classic rock `n' roll tune that may not be as edgy as \\"Fast Lane,\\" but it certainly holds its own in terms of guitar licks.  It isn't the lyrics that make this song; to be quite honest, some almost seem too simple and forced (\\"Some people never come clean/I think you know what I mean\\" & \\"Victim of love, you're just a victim of love/I could be wrong but I'm not\\").  Rather it is the grand total of five part harmonies that compliment the screaming slide guitar part and pounding drums.  It's an easy tune to get stuck in your head, but not a bad one, so don't worry if you start humming it.  On the contrary, the next is a much more mellow song entitled \\"Pretty Maids All in a Row.\\"  Certainly not my personal favorite on the album, it is necessary to the record nevertheless; the lyrics, however, are bittersweet as they remind us that it \\"seems like we've come a long way\\" from the beginning of the album.  Again, I am amazed at how effortlessly the guitar parts fit right into this melancholy ballad.  Short and sweet, \\"Pretty Maids\\" is really an observation on life itself, wondering \\"why must we grow up so fast?\\"  \\"Try and Love Again\\" starts out with a well-played guitar riff and immediately interests the listener because of it.  Henley's voice uses various forms of articulation in his vocals, both clipped and sustained.  The lyrics of this one, similar to \\"Pretty Maids,\\" truthfully tell of the harsh realities of life.  This is one of the stronger songs on the second side, for the sounds of the singer's tortured sorrow seem to emanate from the record itself.  Definitely turn this one up, and sing out loud when this one comes on.  \\"The Last Resort\\" is perhaps one of the best songs in the Eagles' song catalogue and definitely my favorite.  It contradicts perfectly with \\"Hotel\\" in terms of themes and even subject matter.  It tells the story of people moving westward (to the Hotel California?) with \\"hopes and dreams\\" about what America had to offer.  This powerful song is more of an environmental statement, as well as a stand against commercialism, as it depicts humans destructing everything nature has to offer us.  As we \\"satisfy our endless needs and justify our bloody deeds,\\" we end up destroying all the natural beauty of land.  Beginning simply with a piano solo and adding haunting vocals from Henley, it is an important end to the album, as it represents the end of the American Dream.  Clocking in at over seven minutes and filled with moral questions about humanity, \\"The Last Resort\\" still makes one question what the true meaning of \\"paradise\\" is.  This epic's message was important back in the mid-1970s and continues to evoke emotional philosophies about life today in 2011.  I have no doubt in my mind that it will only continue to do so for decades to come.    If you choose to listen to only one Eagles song, let this one be it and listen closely to the lyrics:  \\"Who will provide the grand design?   For what is yours and what is mine?   'Cause there is no more new frontier   We have got to make it here   We satisfy our endless needs and   justify our bloody deeds,   in the name of destiny and the name   of God\\"  Incredibly poignant, \\"The Last Resort\\" definitely helps to make Hotel California an unforgettable album.    Overall, this record may be overrated to some, but there is certainly a reason why people listen to it continuously:  it's an astounding compilation of all types of music, including rock, classical, country, alternative etc.  Perhaps the title song is a little overplayed on the radio, whereas the phenomenal closing song is hardly ever heard anymore.  Nevertheless, I daresay that this is hardly a reason to call such a masterpiece \\"overrated.\\"  Hotel California may be coming up to its fortieth birthday, but it just as could be its fourth, for the themes and musicality within each song continues to remain timeless.

B005PTQPU0|5.0|Being a fan of all Kate's albums, it feels that she has in some ways come full circle, returning to the evocative simplicity of her early work but creating new moods and atmospheres that are fresh and intriguing. After listening to this for a few weeks, it has a special meaning to me. I often play it as a late evening chill out.   Just listening to her in the latest radio interviews, Kate clearly has never seemed happier and her musical Mojo remains undimmed. It seems she already has ideas for her next project and we will be hearing of her more often in the years ahead.  Not so much a collection of songs, but 7 short story 'tone poems'. I welcome Kate's experiment with longer song structures to lose oneself in.  Whilst I would admit this is not an album of catchy seasonal ditties, this album needs patience and repeated listens in order to fully appreciate its beauty. Kate is an artist who produces work on her own terms, free from the shackles of the demands of fan blogs and the music industry establishment.  My general impressions: Shut out the world, put on this album and immerse yourself for an hour uninterrupted in Kate's winter wonderland. Despite only a few listens, new details and elements of the stories are starting to develop in my head. This work is so organic, evocative and dreamy. Forget your troubles and the recession, just escape into Kate's alternative world.  Snowflake: Kate views snow as a substance transforming a landscape or garden with its physical beauty. Kate finds the world so loud and 'lowest common denominator' sometimes, whilst snowflakes are all unique and individual. With repeated listenings, the repeated piano and Bertie motifs become less intrusive and ghostly sounding electronic keyboard details emerge from the mix. Love's Steve Gadds evocation of horses through snowdrifts.   Lake Tahoe: Perhaps the most ambitious song on the album, Kate giving the song an almost classical feel. Stefan Roberts makes a memorable contribution, his openinglines remind me of the end of Hello Earth from the album Hounds of Love. The haunting theme of a dog's almost mythical journey to be reunited with its owner is simple yet atmospheric and moving. This is Kate at her storytelling best.  Misty: I love the almost jazzy feel of Steve Gadd's sunlime drum work. The sequence 'I turn off the light, switch on a starry night' is almost akin to an out of body experience, makes me think of the scene in the film Little Voice, when we first see Jane Horrocks character singing/playing music in her bedroom. 'When I kiss his ice-cream lips' is Kate at her musically bonkers best!!!  Wild Man: The most pop-oriented and perhaps the most immediately accessible track on the album, marking perhaps the beginning of the albums second section. Steve Gadd's hypnotic drums, Andy Fairweather Low's full and throaty chorus and Del Palmer's bells are highlights. Kate's interpretation is an intriguing mix of fear, pity and intrigue for the mythical Yeti.  Snowed In At Wheeler Street: Whilst not particularly well versed in the music of Elton John and despite the controversy evinced by some fans, I think this really does 'work', particularly with Elton's soulful delivery. The idea of the love and loss of friends and lovers by humanity as a universal trait through the centuries is great. Love Kate's increasingly frantic vocal towards the end.  50 Words For Snow: No Kate album is ever complete without a lighter, fun moment. Wacky, this song follows in the tradition of the Big Sky and the song Aerial, whilst keeping to this album's feel. I don't think this track needs to be over-analysed.  Among Angels: A return to the piano-based delivery of the first 3 songs, it is clearly not about snow. Quite simply this song is to me one of the most moving songs she has ever written in her whole career. There is an underlying theme of personal loss, perhaps partly inspired by the recent death of her father, but I feel there is a more general message, about self-belief. We often overlook that the beginnings of a resolution to inner conflicts lies within our own souls.

A summer movie that reminded me how good summer movies used to be before they were taken over by comic book heroes and sequels.  \\"Super 8\\", written and directed by JJ Abrams (MI: III, Star Trek, TV's Lost), is the best summer movie I have seen in at least 2 decades.  It is inspired by Spielberg's ouevre, ad it certainly makes sese that Spielberg produced this as well.  Set in the mid-1970s, Super 8 tells the story of a group of friends who set out to make a zombie movie with a Super 8mm camera.  The kids are around 1-14years old, and the pack is led by Joe Lamb (played beautifully by Joel Courtney).  Joe is a smart, senitive and creative kid who recently lost his mother is an accident.  Courtney is an actor to watch especially watching how deftly he imbues Joe with a fresh-faced sensitivity and wisdom beyond his years withou ever once becoming cloying.  Much the same can be said if the other young actors who fill out his group of friends, among them is Alice (played by Elle Fanning of \\"The Door in the Floor\\"), who is a potential love interest for Joe.  One night while filming their zombie flick, a train derails, nearly killing the lot of them.  This sequence makes up the most exciting and spectacular action scenes and bit of special effects of last summer.  It make the train wreck in \\"The Fugitive\\" look like amateur hour.  It's a sequence that perfectly mixes action, terror, special effects, and performance.  The rest of the film deals with what's in the train and how it affects the town and the characters.  None of which I would dare spoil.  \\"Super 8\\" flooded me with such nostalgia...not just of summer movies past, but of American boyhood, endess summers, and the promise of adventure.  Joe and his friends are at the age where they stand on the precipice of believing in magic and leaving it behind for adulthood. The movie is exciting, often very scary, and very moving.  The adult cast is nicely filled out by \\"Friday Nigh Lights\\" Kyle Chandler as Joe's dad and the town Sherriff, Noah Emmerich (The Walking Dead) as a mysterious military heavy, and Ron Eldard (Deep Impact) as Alice's troubled, alcoholic,and very sad dad.    Director JJ Abrams has made a superior thriller that gives us everything we want in our summer movies.  Action, excitement, lots of humor, brilliant special effects by Industrial Light & Magic, but most of all heart and soul.  Abrams has shown himself as an auteur and not just a brilliant action meastro. In a summer over-stuffed with superhero films with bloated $200 budgets, Abrams made his spectacular tale for a relatively thrifty $50 million.  This goes to show that hundreds of millions does not buy a story a heart.  Only an artist cando wht Abrams has done here.  Super * is Oscar-worthy and the best picture of the year.  \\"Super 8\\" is presented on Blu-ray in a dazzling and flawless MPEG 4 AVC codec.  The PQ is immaculate.  I could not find a single flaw.  Colors are bright and robust, contrast is spot on and edgs are razor sharp.  All of tis is achieved without any digital tampering such as DNR or edge enhancement.  Fine detail is crystalline in presentation.  Textures are fully realized.  The image looks more like a window we could put our hand into rather than flat 2D.  Also worth mentioning, Abrams refused to film in 3D as he feels the market is over-saturated with this gimmick.  He's right and good for him.   The audio is a show-stopper with the first of its kind I have heard at a Dolby TrueHD 7.1.  TrueHD is my favorite audio as it sounds the most realistic.  Surrounds are the most enveloping and chillingly real I have ever hard.  The train derailment is a jaw-drpper.  I felt like I was in the middle of it.  My partner and watchd this sequence 3 times in a row.  It is a sequences which should be playing in all big box stores a it will sell HDTVs and Bluray players in record numbers.  This is why I love this technology.  This is the best audio track I have ever heard.  Completed with crisp, perfect dialogue reproduction...this is the track to top going forward.  This disc is the epitome of reference grade, demo material.  The special features are splendid with terrific cast and filmmaker commentaries, awesome featurettes, among others.  Te packasge includes the BD, a SD DVD and digital copy.  Currently as I write this, \\"Super 8\\" is selling for $19.99.  Buy it now.  Don't bother rentin it.  It also makes a great gift for anyone who loves film...and wants to remmber what summer used to feel like.  Rating: A+

B005ARYEY6|4.0|Let it be known that I consider myself a huge fan of Miles Davis and his music.  When I saw how affordable this box set was, I had to purchase it.  Here are my thoughts:<br /><br />The box set contains three CDs and one DVD.  It also contains a booklet, but there is no designated space to store it in the box set.  This suggests laziness or a lack of forethought on behalf of the company that produced this set.  Buyers should be careful not to let the booklet fall out inadvertently.  Also I had considerable difficulty actually removing the CDs from the case.  I feared I might break the discs in half by mistake because they were so difficult to take out.<br /><br />As for the music itself, the band plays well on all three CDs.  But it is important to make two points about the music.<br /><br />1.  First of all, note that these songs were recorded in 1967 with Miles Davis's second great quintet featuring Herbie Hancock and Wayne Shorter.  Casual jazz fans should be advised that the sound of this quintet is quite different from that of his first great quintet with John Coltrane from the 1950s.  In other words, the playing styles are a bit more experimental (such as rapid tempos, less piano, etc.) and reflect Miles' moving away from traditional bebop.  If you're looking for an easy-listening sound like \\"Kind of Blue\\" or \\"Milestones,\\" this box set is not it.  But if you're looking for a more aggressive sound like \\"ESP\\" or \\"Gingrebread Boy,\\" you will be pleased.<br /><br />2.  I strongly recommend that you check the track listing of the CDs before buying this set.  Even though there are three discs, there are several songs that are played multiple times.  \\"Round Midnight,\\" for example, is played at least three times.  \\"Masqualero\\" is played at least twice.  Serious jazz fans who like studying improvisation will probably be thrilled by this.  But casual jazz fans looking for a more diverse track selection might be disappointed.<br /><br />I have two verdicts on this box set.  Serious students of jazz or serial Miles lovers would likely be pleased by this box set.  There are some interesting arrangements of some of his most famous songs, and the solos are expressive.  However, because of the amount of song repetition, the casual jazz lover might be better off simply buying three individual Miles CDs.  At least that way, you could get a bit more variety regarding the song selection.<br /><br />(review edited on 2-23-12)Serious students of jazz or serial Miles lovers would likely be pleased by this box set. B005KJZDXK|3.0|First off, I love Christmas music. I approached this album with an open and optimistic mind, having just become aware of the talents and charm of She & Him. The song selection is strong, Deschanel's voice is captivating, Ward's playing and singing are also pleasant, and most of the arrangements are very sweet. However, what keeps this project from becoming a classic in the Christmas canon is the production. It appears it was over-produced and engineered by amateurs using Playskool's \\"My First Music Studio\\" play set. The best songs here are simple arrangements, single instrument and Deschanel's voice riding on top such as \\"The Christmas Waltz\\" and \\"The Christmas Song\\". A few of the songs had so many layers of sound they were distracting and Deschanel was buried deep in the mix, such as \\"Christmas Day\\". But perhaps the worst part was the extreme use of reverb. Why, oh why are they performing deep in a cavern? Typically the overuse of reverb in mixing, like too much Auto-Tune, is used by inexperienced producers to cover a less than pleasant voice. But this is a real insult to Deschanel, who has a charming, even intoxicating voice (unless all I've heard has been trickery, which I doubt). So who is the team of Audio Engineer interns who thought this would sound better if we digitally dropped She & Him in a cave? Bad Producer! No pizza for you.     I have always felt that Christmas music should be intended to make us feel the warmth of humanity, the comfort of close friends and family. Christmas recordings like this one should sound like these are old friends sitting in our living room sharing songs from their heart, not belching out of the mouth of a cold, dank cave. IF this project were remixed to put Deschanel not only in front of the band, but also in the room with us, it would become one of my top 20 Christmas albums of the last 50 years. As it is, I can't even listen to it.

B0000046W9|4.0|Though most of the playing on the first three discs is exquisite and poised, I can partly sympathise with those jazz-fans who might find the music a little bland and middle-of-the-road.   However, CD Four is largely given over to Getz's 1963 session with guitarist Laurindo Almeida - and here the saxophonist is far more extrovert and forceful (without ever being inappropriately brutal, needless to say!) His deployment of a wider range of attack and tone-colour - and especially his vivid sequences of staccato notes - give his solos an exhilarating spontaneity, a kick and a bite that elsewhere is often lacking. Almeida's breaks, though not exactly jazz solos, offer beautifully chorded paraphrases of the themes (half of them his own compositions). Steve Kuhn is listed as the pianist on all seven tracks, but I hear him on only three: depending on how indulgent one feels, his contributions will sound either dreamily impressionistic, or inconsequential and unfocussed!   The other discs include the 1962-3 Getz LPs \\"Jazz Samba\\" (with Charlie Byrd), \\"Big Band Bossa Nova\\" (with Gary McFarland's band), \\"Jazz Samba Encore\\" (with Luiz Bonfa) and \\"Getz/Gilberto\\" (with Antonio Carlos Jobim, as well as both Joao and Astrud Gilberto). As long as one resists the temptation to treat it as background music, there is much to savour here: the gently undulating rhythms, the rich harmonies, the fine guitar work of Byrd and Bonfa, the seductively understated vocals from Astrud and Joao, the subtly infectious Brazilian percussion ... and of course Stan's (almost too) perfectly balanced solos.   The booklet explains that the supposed \\"live\\" album from 1964, \\"Getz Au Go Go \\" (with Astrud, and brilliant young Gary Burton on vibes), actually consisted of \\"studio material plus phony applause\\". For this CD set, only genuine live tracks (some previously unissued) are included - though even there a footnote states some of Astrud's vocals were added later!

B004CLYKLI|4.0|I would highly recommend this book for discussion, but I haven't seen the issue of religious background or motivation for excellence often discussed in reviews.  From my perspective, the questions of where the value of success comes from is the most interesting part of the book. Chua grew up nominally Catholic and her husband is Jewish, but she admits that faith-based heritage does not form the driving motivation for the expectations of success she places on her children. As a Christian myself, I often ask why it is that I want my children to succeed academically, in sports, music, or other pursuits. And I do want my children to succeed. I currently teach at an international Christian school, and one of the stereotypes our school constantly struggles against is the perception that Christian schools water down curriculum because they only really care about whether or not you love Jesus. As a parent, I wrestle with the question the book raises of, \\"Why do I desire my children to be successful?\\"  Chua addresses the issue of motivation for success as she observes her highly accomplished sister fight for her life in a battle with leukemia. Her sister despairs most about leaving behind her husband and young children. It made me consider how I allocate my own time, energy, and investment in the future. One of the failures I found in common both with Chua's philosophy and a common Christian approach is the belief that the present is irrelevant compared to the future. For Chua, this means any sacrifice now is worth the future reward of success. For the future-focused Christian, it means interest in the present is often abandoned because of a focus on eternity.   I have personally come to believe that the present and the future are not separate from each other; while delayed gratification can be a virtue, living with a focus that is disconnected from the present time ignores the current trials and suffering of those we are called to care for. As a parent, I hope my children will succeed because they desire to creatively and fully live out the gift of life they have been given. I share Chua's belief that children are born with tremendous potential and capability, and as parents we have a responsibility to help children discover and develop their gifts. But this process should be life-giving, not life-draining. The questions I found myself asking were, \\"Who is this family performing for? For whom are they laboring to develop their gifts and talents? In whose eyes do they desire to be deemed successful?\\" These are the questions I would be interested to see addressed.

1451629184|4.0|Jaycee Dugard was kidnapped at the age of 11 and spent the next eighteen years with her kidnapper and his wife, both of whom had multiple psychological issues.  Considering their problems and the time she was forced to stay with them, it's a miracle that Ms Dugard didn't end up with more issues than she reports in her book.  I find this book incredible and harrowing for several reasons.  First, Ms Dugard wrote it herself - very likely with some assistance from a professional editor, but the fact that she wrote it at all, with or without help, is amazing considering what she went through.  Second, her descriptions of Phillip Garrido and his wife Nancy don't pull any punches.  She doesn't describe the worst of what happened to her (with the exceptions of the rapes), but her hatred of these two individuals is extremely clear, despite her claims of forgiveness to the Garrdios - even though she has to pretend to get along with them and to be happy in order to survive.  Third, and most amazing from my point of view, is the fact that she read the audio version of this book in its entirety.  It's incredible enough that she wrote this book, but I'm sure her therapist, friends, and family all stood by and stayed with her while she did it.  But to read it out loud after she wrote it, to an audience of total strangers?  I'm amazed she was able to do that.  I wouldn't be surprised at all if she didn't break down at several points along the way while she was telling this story.  In fact, I'm sure she did - but you can't tell from the recording.  Finally, to anyone who criticizes the book for its writing style, or the audiobook for the way it's narrated, you need to remember that Jaycee Dugard is not a professional writer, reader, or actor.  She's just an ordinary woman who was caught up in horrific circumstances and lived to tell the tale.  That she was able to tell it at all - both on paper and on audio - speaks volumes for her courage and her intelligence, and I salute her fordoing it.  She's made it clear, now that she's told her story, that all she wants is privacy for her, her daughters, and her extended family.  I sincerely hope that she gets her wish.

1932907009|3.0|The fact that the author wrote 2 sequels to this book proves that even he didn't really believe that this was the last book on screenwriting you'll ever need. I'm not as ardent a follower of this system as some people are, but there is a lot of good info here. Some of his rules are a bit too carved in stone for me, and I don't like the way he implies that if you don't follow them you will fail. He rags on some pretty good movies, like 'Memento', for not following his rules, and justifies it by saying that they didn't make much money compared to the ones he praises. I don't care for the fact that he calls his rules \\"immutable laws\\" and it bothers me that his whole approach, and the justification of his system, and of his criticism of other films, is about money. Yes, movies are a business, yes we all want to make money, but it gets old to me when that becomes your primary driving force, and the test of a movie's quality. Still, as I said, there's a lot of useful/helpful information in here. Ignore the 'immutable' part and his rules make some fine 'guidelines.' The beat-sheet he lays out has helped me plot out two difficult screenplays quickly, and any of his system's points can be very helpful when you run into trouble. Still, I guess the part I dislike is that at times the author comes across to me as kind of arrogant, especially for the guy who wrote 'Stop or My Mom Will Shoot.'<br />As a last note, this book does include some wonderful advise and help for actually selling your screenplay. This wasn't of much help to me as writer/director because I write to make the film myself no to sell to someone else, but if that is what you're looking to do, this is the most helpful book on that subject that I've read so far.

1451627289|2.0|I have just completed reading this, my first King novel. I've not read any of Mr. Kings other books or short stories, primarily because I have had little interest in his type of genre. What changed my mind with regard to 11/22/63 was that the time travel premise interested me, I've been a JFK admirer for many years, and after watching several interviews with Mr. King, he implied that he had spent a great deal of time researching the \\"what ifs\\" of a post 11/22/63 ongoing Kennedy administration. I had great expectations for this book based on what I had heard from Mr. King and of course his reputation.  My expectations were not rewarded. The story begins with a fascinating premise, supports the primary characters with an intriguing initial story line, then slowly falls apart through the second half of the book. By the end of the novel, I put the book down and had to ask the question that any reader should ask an author... was the book worth my time? I thought that my time had ultimately been wasted.   The book is over-long. The promising plot dissolves into a criminologist overview of the Oswald character that can't quite make that man seem real to me the reader. As Oswald develops as a key figure in the novel, the book  becomes labored and depressing, so much so that by the middle of the book, I wanted the story to move to a conclusion which seemed nowhere in sight.   Several key characters appear, then disappear without adding any significant purpose to the story line. I can only suspect that they were added as \\"fillers\\" to extend the book length. The ending by the very nature of the premise insured predictability that King then tried to modify with weak results. The book 's last 150 pages ultimately seemed as though Mr. King had lost interest in this project and wanted to opt-out as conveniently as possible. When finished, I put the book down and wondered...\\"is that all there is?\\" I felt cheated and duped.

B00004RFFS|4.0|\\"Interview with the Vampire: The Vampire Chronicles\\" is a horror drama film, based on the interview confessions of Louis de Pointe du Lac, played by Brad Pitt. The narrative essentially follows Louis and Lestat de Lioncourt (Tom Cruise) and their partnership as vampires. Lestat is the senior of the two and Louis is his protégé. Louis' journey begins in Louisiana, then to France, and he travels much of the world before returning to America. Louis and Lestat have a challenging friendship, as Louis is at first a reluctant vampire, and this leads to many confrontations between the two of them. The narrative is spread over 200-years, which provides for some interesting transformations in terms of Louis and Lestat's philosophies as vampires. Pitt and Cruise both display credible horror genre acting skills.  Nicholas R.W. Henning - Australian Author
